The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.

------------------------------expand_response------------------------------
 The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>

------------------------------rollout_response:------------------------------
[Core idea]: Express x in terms of y and solve for x.
[Full step]: 
The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$. 
Since we are given that $x$ and $y$ are positive real numbers, the factor $(x + y)$ cannot be equal to 0. Therefore, the equation $(x + y) = 0$ is not a solution. The only solution is $(x - 3y) = 0$. We can solve for x by rearranging the equation to obtain $x = 3y$.
</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Substitute the value of x in terms of y into the equation $y^3=x^2$.
[Full step]: 
We have found that $x = 3y$. Now, we can substitute the value of x into the equation $y^3=x^2$ to obtain $y^3 = (3y)^2$. We can simplify the right-hand side of the equation by evaluating $(3y)^2 = 9y^2$.
</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Solve the equation to obtain the value of y.
[Full step]: 
Now, we have $y^3 = 9y^2$. We can rearrange the equation to obtain $y^3 - 9y^2 = 0$. Factoring out $y^2$, we get $y^2(y - 9) = 0$. We know that $y$ is a positive real number, so $y^2$ is not equal to 0. Therefore, the equation $y^2(y - 9) = 0$ implies that $y - 9 = 0$. Solving for $y$, we get $y = 9$.
</step>

<step>

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Factor the quadratic equation in terms of x.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.
</step>

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>

Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
[Core idea]: Use the given equation $y^3=x^2$ to substitute y in terms of x into the expression for x from the previous step.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$, which can be rewritten as $y = x^{\frac{2}{3}}$. Now, we substitute this expression for y into the two possible solutions for x: $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$. Substituting $y = x^{\frac{2}{3}}$ into $x = 3y$, we get $x = 3x^{\frac{2}{3}}$. To solve for x, we can rearrange this equation as $x - 3x^{\frac{2}{3}} = 0$. Multiplying both sides by $x^{-\frac{2}{3}}$, we obtain $x^{\frac{2}{3}} - 3 = 0$. Adding 3 to both sides, we get $x^{\frac{2}{3}} = 3$. Cubing both sides, we have $x^2 = 27$. Taking the cube root of both sides, we get $x = 3\sqrt[3]{3}$. Substituting $y = x^{\frac{2}{3}}$ into $x = -y$, we get $x = -x^{\frac{2}{3}}$. To solve for x, we can rearrange this equation as $x + x^{\frac{2}{3}} = 0$. Multiplying both sides by $x^{-\frac{2}{3}}$, we obtain $x^{\frac{1}{3}} + 1 = 0$. This equation cannot be solved for a positive real number x.
</step>
<step>
[Core idea]: Use the value of x to find the value of y.
[Full step]: We found that $x = 3\sqrt[3]{3}$. We can substitute this value of x into the equation $y = x^{\frac{2}{3}}$ to solve for y: $y = (3\sqrt[3]{3})^{\frac{2}{3}} = 3$. Now that we have the values of x and y, we can find $x + y$.
</step>
<step>
[Core idea]: Find the sum of x and y.
[Full step]: We have x = $3\sqrt[3]{3}$ and y = 3. Therefore, $x + y = 3\sqrt[3]{3} + 3$.
</step>

Now, we can simplify the expression for $x + y$ by rationalizing the denominator. To do this, we multiply the numerator and denominator by $\sqrt[3]{3^2} = 3^{\frac{2}{3}} = \sqrt[3]{9}$.

<step>
[Full step]: We have $x + y = \frac{3\sqrt[3]{3} \cdot \sqrt[3]{9}}{\sqrt[3]{9}} = \frac{3\sqrt[3]{27}}{\sqrt[3]{9}} = \frac{3 \cdot 3}{\sqrt[3]{9}} = \frac{9}{\sqrt[3]{9}}$. To rationalize the denominator, we multiply the numerator and denominator by $\sqrt[3]{9^2} = 9^{\frac{2}{3}} = \sqrt[3]{81}$. This gives us $x + y = \frac{9 \cdot \sqrt[3]{81}}{\sqrt[3]{9} \cdot \sqrt[3]{81}} = \frac{9 \cdot \sqrt[3]{81}}{\sqrt[3]{729}} = \frac{9 \cdot 3}{9} = \boxed{3}

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Factor the quadratic equation in terms of x.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.
</step>

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Use the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to express x in terms of y and substitute in one of the solutions for x from the quadratic equation to eliminate x and solve for y.
[Full step]: Since we have $x^2 = y^3$, we can substitute $x=3y$ into this equation to get $(3y)^2=y^3$. Expanding the left-hand side, we have $9y^2=y^3$. We can rearrange the equation to get $y^3-9y^2=0$. Factoring out a $y^2$, we obtain $y^2(y-9)=0$. This gives us two possible solutions for y: $y^2=0$ or $y-9=0$. Since the problem states that y is a positive real number, the solution $y^2=0$ is not valid. Therefore, we have $y-9=0$, which gives us $y=9$.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{15}
</step>
</think>

</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. </step>
Step 3: <step>

[Full step]: Since $(y-x)^2 = 4y^2$, we know that $(y-x)^2$ is nonnegative. Thus, $y-x$ is either nonpositive or nonnegative, which implies that either $y \geq x$ or $y \leq x$. However, since $y^3 = x^2$, we know that $y$ and $x$ must both be positive. Therefore, we must have $y > x$. Since $y > x$ and $y^3 = x^2$, we have $x = \frac{y^3}{y} = y^2$. If we substitute this value of x into the equation $(y-x)^2 = 4y^2$, we obtain $(y-y^2)^2 = 4y^2$, which simplifies to $(1-y)^2 = 4$.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{1+y}

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Substitute the solutions for x from the quadratic equation into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to obtain a system of equations and solve for the values of x and y.
[Full step]: Since we obtained two possible solutions for x, namely $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$, we can substitute these values into the equation $y^3=x^2$. Let's start with $x = 3y$. Substituting this value into the equation $y^3=x^2$, we get $y^3 = (3y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we obtain $y = 9$. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 9 into the equation $x = 3y$. This gives us $x = 3(9) = 27$. Next, let's substitute $x = -y$ into the equation $y^3=x^2$. We get $y^3 = (-y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = y^2$. We can rewrite this equation as $y^3 - y^2 = 0$. Factoring out y^2, we obtain $y^2(y - 1) = 0$. Since y^2 is positive, we can divide both sides of the equation by y^2, and we get $y - 1 = 0$. Solving for y, we have y = 1. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 1 into the equation $x = -y$. This gives us $x = -1$. Now that we have the values of x and y for both cases, we can find the sum x + y. By substituting the values of x and y, we have x + y = 27 + 9 for the first case, and x + y = -1 + 1 for the second case. Therefore, we get x + y = 36 for the first case and x + y = 0 for the second case. Since the problem states that x and y are positive real numbers, we can eliminate the second case. Thus, x + y = 36.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}
</step>

Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Substitute the solutions for x from the quadratic equation into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to obtain a system of equations and solve for the values of x and y.
[Full step]: Since we obtained two possible solutions for x, namely $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$, we can substitute these values into the equation $y^3=x^2$. Let's start with $x = 3y$. Substituting this value into the equation $y^3=x^2$, we get $y^3 = (3y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we obtain $y = 9$. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 9 into the equation $x = 3y$. This gives us $x = 3(9) = 27$. Next, let's substitute $x = -y$ into the equation $y^3=x^2$. We get $y^3 = (-y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = y^2$. We can rewrite this equation as $y^3 - y^2 = 0$. Factoring out y^2, we obtain $y^2(y - 1) = 0$. Since y^2 is positive, we can divide both sides of the equation by y^2, and we get $y - 1 = 0$. Solving for y, we have y = 1. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 1 into the equation $x = -y$. This gives us $x = -1$. Now that we have the values of x and y for both cases, we can find the sum x + y. By substituting the values of x and y, we have x + y = 27 + 9 for the first case, and x + y = -1 + 1 for the second case. Therefore, we get x + y = 36 for the first case and x + y = 0 for the second case. Since the problem states that x and y are positive real numbers, we can eliminate the second case. Thus, x + y = 36.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}
</step>
</think>

------------------------------pairwise_prompt------------------------------
Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Use the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to express x in terms of y and substitute in one of the solutions for x from the quadratic equation to eliminate x and solve for y.
[Full step]: Since we have $x^2 = y^3$, we can substitute $x=3y$ into this equation to get $(3y)^2=y^3$. Expanding the left-hand side, we have $9y^2=y^3$. We can rearrange the equation to get $y^3-9y^2=0$. Factoring out a $y^2$, we obtain $y^2(y-9)=0$. This gives us two possible solutions for y: $y^2=0$ or $y-9=0$. Since the problem states that y is a positive real number, the solution $y^2=0$ is not valid. Therefore, we have $y-9=0$, which gives us $y=9$.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{15}
</step>

[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Factor the quadratic equation in terms of x.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.
</step>

[Core idea]: The given problem states that $y^3 = x^2$, so we can substitute this expression for $x^2$ in the factored equation to solve for y.

[Full step]: We can substitute $y^3$ for $x^2$ in the factored equation $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$. This gives us the equation $(y^3 - 3y)(y + y) = 0$. We can simplify this equation by multiplying the terms: $(y^3 - 3y)(2y) = 0$. This equation can be further simplified to $2y^4 - 6y^2 = 0$.

</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Factor the resulting equation to find the possible values of y.

[Full step]: The equation $2y^4 - 6y^2 = 0$ can be factored by taking out a common factor of $2y^2$. This gives us $2y^2(y^2 - 3) = 0$. We can factor the difference of squares inside the parentheses: $2y^2(y - \sqrt{3})(y + \sqrt{3}) = 0$.

</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Use the zero product property to find the possible values of y.

[Full step]: Since the equation $2y^2(y - \sqrt{3})(y + \sqrt{3}) = 0$ has three factors, we can apply the zero product property to find the possible values of y. The product is equal to zero if and only if at least one of the factors is equal to zero. Therefore, we have three cases: $y^2 = 0$, $y - \sqrt{3} = 0$, or $y + \sqrt{3} = 0$. We can solve each of these equations to find the possible values of y.

</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Solve the equation $y - \sqrt{3} = 0$ to find the value of y.

[Full step]: Solving the equation $y - \sqrt{3} = 0$ for y, we get $y = \sqrt{3}$. This is the first possible value of y.

</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Solve the equation $y + \sqrt{3} = 0$ to find the value of y.

[Full step]: Solving the equation $y + \sqrt{3} = 0$ for y, we get $y = -\sqrt{3}$. This is the second possible value of y.

</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Substitute $y = \sqrt{3}$ into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to find the value of x.

[Full step]: Substituting $y = \sqrt{3}$ into the equation $y^3 = x^2$, we get $(\sqrt{3})^3 = x^2$. Simplifying this equation, we have $3\sqrt{3} = x^2$. Taking the square root of both sides, we get $x = \pm \sqrt{3\sqrt{3}}$. This can be simplified to $x = \pm \sqrt{3}\sqrt[4]{3}$.

</step>

<step>
[Core idea]: Substitute $y = -\sqrt{3}$ into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to find the value of x.

[Full step]: Substituting $y = -\sqrt{3}$ into the equation $y^3 = x^2$, we get $(-\sqrt{3})^3 = x^2$. Simplifying this equation, we have $-3\sqrt{3} = x^2$. Taking the square root of both sides, we get $x = \pm i\sqrt{3\sqrt{3}}$. This can be simplified to $x = \pm i\sqrt{3}\sqrt[4]{3}$.

</step>

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Factor the quadratic equation in terms of x.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Use the quadratic formula to solve for x, considering x as the variable and y as a constant.
[Full step]: Since we factored the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$, we can use the zero-product property to find the values of x. The equation is true if either factor equals zero. So, we have two cases: $x - 3y = 0$ or $x + y = 0$. We can solve these equations to find the possible values of x. If $x - 3y = 0$, we can solve for x by adding $3y$ to both sides, which gives us $x = 3y$. If $x + y = 0$, we can solve for x by subtracting y from both sides, which gives us $x = -y$.

Step 1: <step>

[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Factor the quadratic equation in terms of x.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Use the factored form to express x in terms of y and substitute it into the equation y^3 = x^2.
[Full step]: We have the factored form $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$. This means that either $x - 3y = 0$ or $x + y = 0$. If $x - 3y = 0$, then $x = 3y$. If $x + y = 0$, then $x = -y$. We can substitute both expressions into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to find the values of y. For $x = 3y$, we have $y^3 = (3y)^2$, which simplifies to $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we get $y = 9$. For $x = -y$, we have $y^3 = (-y)^2$, which simplifies to $y^3 = y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we get $y = 1$.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}
</step>
</think>

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Factor the quadratic equation in terms of x.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation in terms of x is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can factor this quadratic equation by finding two numbers whose product is $-3y^2$ and whose sum is $-2y$. The numbers are $-3y$ and $y$, because $(-3y)(y) = -3y^2$ and $(-3y) + y = -2y$. So, we can factor the quadratic equation as $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Use the factored form to express x in terms of y and substitute it into the equation y^3 = x^2.
[Full step]: We have the factored form $(x - 3y)(x + y) = 0$. This means that either $x - 3y = 0$ or $x + y = 0$. If $x - 3y = 0$, then $x = 3y$. If $x + y = 0$, then $x = -y$. We can substitute both expressions into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to find the values of y. For $x = 3y$, we have $y^3 = (3y)^2$, which simplifies to $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we get $y = 9$. For $x = -y$, we have $y^3 = (-y)^2$, which simplifies to $y^3 = y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we get $y = 1$.

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Attempt to substitute y from the first equation $y^3=x^2$ into the rearranged equation in step 1 to eliminate y.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$ and $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the first equation to express $x^2$ in terms of $y$, which is $x^2 = y^3$. Now, let's substitute $x^2 = y^3$ into the second equation. We get $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. 

</step>

[Core idea]: Attempt to factor the quadratic equation $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]:
------------------------------expand_response------------------------------
 We have $y^3=x^2$ and $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the second equation to isolate y: $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0 \Rightarrow y^3 - 3y^2 = 2yx \Rightarrow y^2(y-3) = 2yx \Rightarrow y^2 = 2x$ since $y\neq 3$ (otherwise, $x^2=0$ and $y^3=0$ would imply that $x=y=0$, which contradicts the assumption that $x$ and $y$ are positive real numbers). Now, substituting $y^2 = 2x$ into $y^3=x^2$, we get $(2x)^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2 \Rightarrow 2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2 \Rightarrow 2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2.$ If $x=0$, then $2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=0 ^{\frac{3}{2}}=0$ and $x^2=0$, the equation holds, but this contradicts the fact that $x$ is a positive real number. Therefore, we can divide both sides by $x^{\frac{3}{2}}$, giving us $2^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^{\frac{1}{2}} \Rightarrow x=2^3 = 8$. Substituting $x=8$ into $y^2=2x$, we get $y^2=2(8)=16$, and since $x$ and $y$ are positive, $y=4$. The sum of x and y is then $x+y=8+4=12$.
</step>

[Check terminal] 
The final answer to the question is \boxed{12}.

------------------------------expand_response------------------------------
 We have $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. Since $x^2 = y^3$, we can substitute this into the equation to obtain $y^3 - 2y\sqrt{y^3} - 3y^2 = 0$. We can simplify this to $y^3 - 2y^{\frac{3}{2}} - 3y^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation, we get $y^3 - 3y^2 - 2y^{\frac{3}{2}} = 0$. We can factor out $y^2$ from the first two terms to obtain $y^2(y - 3) - 2y^{\frac{3}{2}} = 0$. Now, we can rewrite $y^2$ as $y^{\frac{3}{2}} \cdot y^{\frac{-1}{2}}$ to obtain $y^{\frac{3}{2}}(y^{\frac{1}{2}} - 3y^{-\frac{1}{2}}) = 0$. This can be written as $y^{\frac{3}{2}}(y^{\frac{1}{2}} - 3y^{-\frac{1}{2}}) = 0$. Multiplying through by $y^{\frac{1}{2}}$ to eliminate the fraction, we get $y^2(y - 3y^{-1}) = 0$. Now, we can simplify this to $y^3 - 3y = 0$. Rearranging the equation, we have $y^3 - 3y = 0$. We can factor out $y$ from the left-hand side to obtain $y(y^2 - 3) = 0$. Therefore, we have two possible values for y: $y = 0$ and $y^2 - 3 = 0$. Since $y$ is a positive real number, we only consider the second equation. Solving for $y$, we get $y^2 = 3$. Taking the square root of both sides, we get $y = \pm \sqrt{3}$. Since $y$ is a positive real number, we have $y = \sqrt{3}$.
</step>

[Core idea]: Substitute $y = \sqrt{3}$ into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to find the value of $x$.
[Full step]: Now that we have found $y = \sqrt{3}$, we can substitute this value into the equation $y^3 = x^2$. We get $(\sqrt{3})^3 = x^2$. Simplifying, we have $3\sqrt{3} = x^2$. Taking the square root of both sides, we get $x = \pm\sqrt{3\sqrt{3}}$. We can simplify this to $x = \pm\sqrt{3}\sqrt{\sqrt{3}} = \pm 3^{\frac{3}{4}}$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Since $x$ is a positive real number, we take the positive root of $x = 3^{\frac{3}{4}}$.
[Full step]: $x$ is a positive real number. Therefore, we take the positive root of $x = 3^{\frac{3}{4}}$, which gives us $x = 3^{\frac{3}{4}}$. 
</step>

Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Attempt to substitute y from the first equation $y^3=x^2$ into the rearranged equation in step 1 to eliminate y.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$ and $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the first equation to express $x^2$ in terms of $y$, which is $x^2 = y^3$. Now, let's substitute $x^2 = y^3$ into the second equation. We get $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. 

</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the second equation to express y in terms of x and substitute into the first equation.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$ and $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the second equation to isolate y: $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0 \Rightarrow y^3 - 3y^2 = 2yx \Rightarrow y^2(y-3) = 2yx \Rightarrow y^2 = 2x$ since $y\neq 3$ (otherwise, $x^2=0$ and $y^3=0$ would imply that $x=y=0$, which contradicts the assumption that $x$ and $y$ are positive real numbers). Now, substituting $y^2 = 2x$ into $y^3=x^2$, we get $(2x)^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2 \Rightarrow 2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2 \Rightarrow 2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2.$ If $x=0$, then $2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=0 ^{\frac{3}{2}}=0$ and $x^2=0$, the equation holds, but this contradicts the fact that $x$ is a positive real number. Therefore, we can divide both sides by $x^{\frac{3}{2}}$, giving us $2^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^{\frac{1}{2}} \Rightarrow x=2^3 = 8$. Substituting $x=8$ into $y^2=2x$, we get $y^2=2(8)=16$, and since $x$ and $y$ are positive, $y=4$. The sum of x and y is then $x+y=8+4=12$.

</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Attempt to substitute y from the first equation $y^3=x^2$ into the rearranged equation in step 1 to eliminate y.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$ and $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the first equation to express $x^2$ in terms of $y$, which is $x^2 = y^3$. Now, let's substitute $x^2 = y^3$ into the second equation. We get $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.

Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the second equation to express y in terms of x and substitute into the first equation.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$ and $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the second equation to isolate y: $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0 \Rightarrow y^3 - 3y^2 = 2yx \Rightarrow y^2(y-3) = 2yx \Rightarrow y^2 = 2x$ since $y\neq 3$ (otherwise, $x^2=0$ and $y^3=0$ would imply that $x=y=0$, which contradicts the assumption that $x$ and $y$ are positive real numbers). Now, substituting $y^2 = 2x$ into $y^3=x^2$, we get $(2x)^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2 \Rightarrow 2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2 \Rightarrow 2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^2.$ If $x=0$, then $2^{\frac{3}{2}}x^{\frac{3}{2}}=0 ^{\frac{3}{2}}=0$ and $x^2=0$, the equation holds, but this contradicts the fact that $x$ is a positive real number. Therefore, we can divide both sides by $x^{\frac{3}{2}}$, giving us $2^{\frac{3}{2}}=x^{\frac{1}{2}} \Rightarrow x=2^3 = 8$. Substituting $x=8$ into $y^2=2x$, we get $y^2=2(8)=16$, and since $x$ and $y$ are positive, $y=4$. The sum of x and y is then $x+y=8+4=12$.

Therefore, we have $y^2 = 0$ or $y - 1 = 0$. If $y^2 = 0$, then we have $y = 0$, which we have already solved. If $y - 1 = 0$, then we have $y = 1$. Substituting this value into the equation $y^3 = x^2$, we get $1^3 = x^2$, which gives us $x^2 = 1$, and then we have $x = 1$ or $x = -1$. Since we are looking for the positive real numbers $x$ and $y$, we choose $x = 1$.

</step>

[Full step]: We have two pairs of values for x and

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Attempt to substitute y from the first equation $y^3=x^2$ into the rearranged equation in step 1 to eliminate y.
[Full step]: We have $y^3=x^2$ and $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange the first equation to express $x^2$ in terms of $y$, which is $x^2 = y^3$. Now, let's substitute $x^2 = y^3$ into the second equation. We get $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. 

</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Substitute $x^2 = y^3$ into the rearranged equation and solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: We have $y^3 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. Since $x^2 = y^3$, we can substitute this into the equation to obtain $y^3 - 2y\sqrt{y^3} - 3y^2 = 0$. We can simplify this to $y^3 - 2y^{\frac{3}{2}} - 3y^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation, we get $y^3 - 3y^2 - 2y^{\frac{3}{2}} = 0$. We can factor out $y^2$ from the first two terms to obtain $y^2(y - 3) - 2y^{\frac{3}{2}} = 0$. Now, we can rewrite $y^2$ as $y^{\frac{3}{2}} \cdot y^{\frac{-1}{2}}$ to obtain $y^{\frac{3}{2}}(y^{\frac{1}{2}} - 3y^{-\frac{1}{2}}) = 0$. This can be written as $y^{\frac{3}{2}}(y^{\frac{1}{2}} - 3y^{-\frac{1}{2}}) = 0$. Multiplying through by $y^{\frac{1}{2}}$ to eliminate the fraction, we get $y^2(y - 3y^{-1}) = 0$. Now, we can simplify this to $y^3 - 3y = 0$. Rearranging the equation, we have $y^3 - 3y = 0$. We can factor out $y$ from the left-hand side to obtain $y(y^2 - 3) = 0$. Therefore, we have two possible values for y: $y = 0$ and $y^2 - 3 = 0$. Since $y$ is a positive real number, we only consider the second equation. Solving for $y$, we get $y^2 = 3$. Taking the square root of both sides, we get $y = \pm \sqrt{3}$. Since $y$ is a positive real number, we have $y = \sqrt{3}$.
</step>

------------------------------final_prompt------------------------------
Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Substitute the solutions for x from the quadratic equation into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to obtain a system of equations and solve for the values of x and y.
[Full step]: Since we obtained two possible solutions for x, namely $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$, we can substitute these values into the equation $y^3=x^2$. Let's start with $x = 3y$. Substituting this value into the equation $y^3=x^2$, we get $y^3 = (3y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we obtain $y = 9$. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 9 into the equation $x = 3y$. This gives us $x = 3(9) = 27$. Next, let's substitute $x = -y$ into the equation $y^3=x^2$. We get $y^3 = (-y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = y^2$. We can rewrite this equation as $y^3 - y^2 = 0$. Factoring out y^2, we obtain $y^2(y - 1) = 0$. Since y^2 is positive, we can divide both sides of the equation by y^2, and we get $y - 1 = 0$. Solving for y, we have y = 1. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 1 into the equation $x = -y$. This gives us $x = -1$. Now that we have the values of x and y for both cases, we can find the sum x + y. By substituting the values of x and y, we have x + y = 27 + 9 for the first case, and x + y = -1 + 1 for the second case. Therefore, we get x + y = 36 for the first case and x + y = 0 for the second case. Since the problem states that x and y are positive real numbers, we can eliminate the second case. Thus, x + y = 36.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}
</step>

Thus, x + y = 36.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}

------------------------------final_prompt------------------------------
Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Substitute the solutions for x from the quadratic equation into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to obtain a system of equations and solve for the values of x and y.
[Full step]: Since we obtained two possible solutions for x, namely $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$, we can substitute these values into the equation $y^3=x^2$. Let's start with $x = 3y$. Substituting this value into the equation $y^3=x^2$, we get $y^3 = (3y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we obtain $y = 9$. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 9 into the equation $x = 3y$. This gives us $x = 3(9) = 27$. Next, let's substitute $x = -y$ into the equation $y^3=x^2$. We get $y^3 = (-y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = y^2$. We can rewrite this equation as $y^3 - y^2 = 0$. Factoring out y^2, we obtain $y^2(y - 1) = 0$. Since y^2 is positive, we can divide both sides of the equation by y^2, and we get $y - 1 = 0$. Solving for y, we have y = 1. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 1 into the equation $x = -y$. This gives us $x = -1$. Now that we have the values of x and y for both cases, we can find the sum x + y. By substituting the values of x and y, we have x + y = 27 + 9 for the first case, and x + y = -1 + 1 for the second case. Therefore, we get x + y = 36 for the first case and x + y = 0 for the second case. Since the problem states that x and y are positive real numbers, we can eliminate the second case. Thus, x + y = 36.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}
</step>

Substituting this value into the equation $y^3=x^2$, we get $y^3 = (3y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = 9y^2$. Dividing both sides by $y^2$, we obtain $y = 9$. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 9 into the equation $x = 3y$. This gives us $x = 3(9) = 27$. Next, let's substitute $x = -y$ into the equation $y^3=x^2$. We get $y^3 = (-y)^2$. Simplifying the right-hand side, we have $y^3 = y^2$. We can rewrite this equation as $y^3 - y^2 = 0$. Factoring out y^2, we obtain $y^2(y - 1) = 0$. Since y^2 is positive, we can divide both sides of the equation by y^2, and we get $y - 1 = 0$. Solving for y, we have y = 1. Since we want to check if this value of y is valid, we substitute y = 1 into the equation $x = -y$. This gives us $x = -1$. Now that we have the values of x and y for both cases, we can find the sum x + y. By substituting the values of x and y, we have x + y = 27 + 9 for the first case, and x + y = -1 + 1 for the second case. Therefore, we get x + y = 36 for the first case and x + y = 0 for the second case. Since the problem states that x and y are positive real numbers, we can eliminate the second case. Thus, x + y = 36.


The final answer to the question is \boxed{36}
</step>

Question: Positive real numbers $x$ and $y$ satisfy $y^3=x^2$ and $(y-x)^2=4y^2$. What is $x+y$?

Step 1: <step>
[Core idea]: Rearrange the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$ to solve for x in terms of y.
[Full step]: Rearranging the equation $(y-x)^2=4y^2$, we get $(y-x)^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Expanding the left-hand side, we obtain $y^2 - 2yx + x^2 - 4y^2 = 0$. Combining like terms, we have $-3y^2 - 2yx + x^2 = 0$. Rearranging the equation to isolate x, we get $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can rearrange this equation into a quadratic equation in terms of x: $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$.
</step>
Step 2: <step>
[Core idea]: Solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula.
[Full step]: The quadratic equation is $x^2 - 2yx - 3y^2 = 0$. We can use the quadratic formula to solve for x: $x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2-4ac}}{2a}$. Here, a = 1, b = -2y, and c = -3y^2. Plugging these values into the formula, we get $x = \frac{-(-2y) \pm \sqrt{(-2y)^2-4(1)(-3y^2)}}{2(1)}$. Simplifying the expression under the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{4y^2+12y^2}}{2}$. Combining the like terms under the square root, we get $x = \frac{2y \pm \sqrt{16y^2}}{2}$. Simplifying the square root, we have $x = \frac{2y \pm 4y}{2}$. This gives us two possible solutions for x: $x = \frac{2y + 4y}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2y - 4y}{2}$. Simplifying these expressions, we obtain $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$.
</step>
Step 3: <step>
[Core idea]: Substitute the solutions for x from the quadratic equation into the equation $y^3 = x^2$ to obtain a system of equations and solve for the values of x and y.
[Full step]: Since we obtained two possible solutions for x, namely $x = 3y$ and $x = -y$, we can substitute these values into the equation $y^3=x^2$. Let's start with $x = 3y$.
